RATING: 7.5
Very light set collection and route-planning, but has some light strategy to it.

I had to buy this game mainly for the wife, as it seemed to be a non-confrontational, relatively light game, with a bit of luck that played well with 2.  Well the game certainly fit all of those criteria, and it gets bonus marks, as it actually has some subtle strategy to it, and it's a game that will get regular playtime.

If you like games like Elfenland, where you have to plan out your routes, and Showmanager, where you have to manage your hand carefully (plus it also has the "clear the board" aspect to it), and if you like variable player powers (which I certainly do), then this is a solid lightweight game for 2-4 players.  Nothing terribly original, but it works.  The theme neither adds to nor detracts from the game, except maybe if you're from Germany.

If this game ends up winning the SdJ (turns out it did, surprise, surprise), it's more a reflection of the weakness of the award rather than the greatness of this game.  That said, this is still a fairly safe and solid purchase, assuming you know you're not getting a "gamer's game".  

Own the [boardgame=27690]Power & Glory[/boardgame], [boardgame=33261]All Roads Lead to Rome[/boardgame], and [boardgame=25293]Courier[/boardgame] expansions.

The Power & Glory expansion adds a new map which is great for replay value; you can either play with the original rules or with a new, significant rule change (horses).  I only play with the original rules, as the new rule change removes one of the main aspects of the game (tension).

RATING: 5
Seems a bit too easy - the basic strategy that seems to work best (at least in two player games): go for the "all-but-white" 6-point tile, even not dropping houses on white even, if you can. Start on the left side, since it'll be easier to drop more houses on a five-card route. Remember that Lodz, Innsbruck, and Sigmaringen are the most important cards to get for this - so hoard them if you're opponents trying to get at them and you've got the space in your hand. Once you've gotten this, keep filling in to get the small region tiles. Finally, the reason this works so well, you can dump a ton of houses into white in one route (and get a 5/6/7 tile as well).

RATING: 8
Thurn and Taxis is a surprisingly fun family game. Compared to Ticket to Ride, it's somewhat more complex, but it is also less cutthroat while still having a good feeling of competition. For people who like harsh competition Thurn and Taxis will probably feel boring, but for people who sometimes feel frustrated while playing Ticket to Ride (after seeing another player take the route they wanted), this is a game I would recommend trying.

RATING: 3
Kind of like Ticket to Ride (I guess) in that you collect sets of cards to make routes (kind of) and get points, but is missing a few key things I like about Ticket to Ride:
-Trains. Trains are [i]way[/i] cooler than mail carriages.
-Familiar cities. A map of the US is just cool and more familiar.
-Easier setup and simpler rules.
-Direct interaction with other players. In TtR, you compete for tracks and routes and block other players from using them. You can't do anything like that in Thurn and Taxis.
-A cool theme. Did I mention trains are cooler than mail carriages? Maybe if the game was rethemed as "Turnin' Taxis" and set in New York City and players build up taxi cab companies, I'd like it more.
-Tension; it just doesn't have any.
-It's really freaking lame, by comparison.

RATING: 5
My feel of the game is that this is Ticket to Ride Solitaire. I'd like to have more player interaction. 

2016
This is a game in which the starting player has a significant advantage over the others. Basically the game is a race to reach certain goals and there is no catch-up mechanism. There is very little interaction since all you can do to prevent your opponents from reaching the goals before you is to pick up the cards they want. In an optimal situation you want the same cards but most of the time blocking is a move wasted, thus resulting in status quo or a third player gaining an edge. Of course you can play well or bad, but there is little you can do about the luck of draw.

RATING: 7
In Thurn and Taxis, players build post office routes across Bavaria and the regions around, collecting bonus points in various ways. The board shows a map of all the towns, with roads leading from each one to some its neighbors. There are various colored regions around the board, most with one or two towns, and a large region with all the Bavarian towns in the center. With the right strategy, this game lets players build routes and stagecoaches, found offices in cities and expand into new countries. All the while making good use of public departments and having a lucky hand when planning new routes.

:thumbsup: There is a unique puzzle to work through in terms of the routes and bonus tiles with every play.
:thumbsup: The lovely artwork and design create a cozy feeling to ensure that there is little stress.

[microbadge=30754][c] [/c]Repeated shuffling during setup can feel tiresome, even though this is when strategies can be thought through.
[microbadge=30754][c] [/c]The order of the cards is vital, yet equally important is learning what to do with suboptimal postal routes.

:thumbsdown: Once the player has mastered the decision making process, the level of difficulty feels noticeably lighter.
:thumbsdown: Continuous bad luck can immediately lead to lost points, with no way to mitigate the damage.

RATING: 7.5
FAMILY, STRATEGY

Thurn and Taxis feels like a more complex version of Ticket to Ride. Players build postal networks throughout Austria, Switzerland, Germany, and Poland. There isn't much in the way of defensive play or direct confrontation here--the confrontation is the competition to be as efficient and fast as possible to win. Maggie and I soon figured out not to focus on the carriage cards, but the bonus tiles.

Recommended with 2-4 players (best 3-4). 60 mins.

RATING: 7.5
This game involves in linking postal routes in Europe.  With cards of cities in hand, players placed cards of adjacent cities to form their postal routes.  A minimum of 3 cities must be connected before players can score and proceed to start a new route.  

4 super powers are their to help players in the game and they are allowed to use 1 of them in each round of the game.

There are various ways to score in the game, link up cities of the same region, be one of the first players to link up at least 5 cities, or the first one to end the game.  A game that combines luck, strategies and planning.

Good points: Easy to play.  This game requires some advance thinking during each round.  Every decision made by players are crucial to the game.

Bad points: After a few rounds, players might be able to get the formula to winning the game and have an advantage in winning the game over other players.  It can be frustrating that players cannot get their necessary city cards for connecting purposes but this is also the challenge of this game.

This game is very similar to Ticket to Ride, but I would prefer this to Ticket to Ride.  This game includes the 4 super powers which players can make more decisions to determine the game.  However in Ticket to Ride, it is more of a luck based game compared to Thurn and Taxis.

RATING: 7.5
The theme is pasted on, but there's no glue bleeding through anywhere! The board and bits are simply beautiful. It is not, by any stretch of the imagination, a deep game (either in rules complexity or in strategy), but some strategic thought is required. Yes, there is little player interaction at first glance, but that all depends on how you choose to play it: you MUST constantly be aware of what your opponents are up to. T&T goes by quickly, and this makes it nicely tense.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
2-4(3,4)

Clearly, similar "route-building" gameplay to TtR, but it's hard for me to say which I like better. T&T has no blocking of routes, of roles, of nearly anything with, I guess, the exception of taking a card that your opponent would clearly want. But even so, with the Administrator, it seems like you can essentially ensure that you'll have a play. If you don't, and have to scrap a route, I'd have to imagine that it's essentially game over (soft player elimination).

And then, even if you do take Administrator (and likely "save" your route) you probably lost a crucial iota because your turn was slightly sub-optimal (by not taking or placing two cards). Games with a "bail-out" mechanism in which you make a losing move but the game structures it such that you don't "feel" like its a losing move annoy me. Make losing moves obvious, and losing! I hate the subterfuge of taking a bad move, but the game hand-waving it to make it seem like it wasn't so bad. To be fair, though, I can see taking Administrator a few times each play-through ... it's relying on Administrator that can lose you a game.

ANYway, this is pretty much pure logistical optimization. I guess there are interesting decisions: whether to rush the carriages or get house coverage. House placement too often feels too straightforward, and I miss blocking. Still, you do need to be aware of everybody's status on routes and carriages, but I suspect your best move will often be just to optimize your own routes. I'm not sure how long I'll go with this one, as it's awkwardly in between a solid gateway (which TtR accomplishes famously) and heavier games (I currently lack a group for these, but I have little issue getting a brain-burner to the table against my wife).

RATING: 4
Was initially pretty impressed with this game as it sported some nice components and rules which could be easily taught to family members and non-gaming friends.  However after repeated plays the gameplay just doesn't hold up for me anymore.  The choices presented aren't really all that difficult nor compelling as its fairly obvious to each player about which official to use, what cards to collect, where to extend your route and when to call your route in for points/ bonuses.  Overall, a decent race/ press your luck route connection game which some may favor due to the absence of player confrontation.  For a better family route building experience with added excitement, tension, player interaction and even simpler rules, see Ticket to Ride.  Or for a route connection game which has a lot more going on with just as complex rules as this, see On the Underground.

RATING: 9
{100  plays, 2-4 players} I suspect my rating could drop a point in a year or so, after we all stop playing this one as much.  But for now, I dig the route-planning puzzley part, the second-guess your opponents part, the offensive vs. defensive card draw part, the hand management part, and the long routes vs. short route/cartwright part. *** UPDATE 10/07/07 [initial rating: 8] ***

Ha.  My suspicions were wrong.  Mostly due to how much I enjoy this as a 2-player on BSW.  This game is still terrific, still has a nice balance between building long routes for the extra points or short routes for tempo, and still offers those wonderful decisions of how best to deal with the cards you are presented with - both offensively and defensively.  The only reason this isn't a 10 for me is the four-player game: the length there doesn't quite match the depth for me, though obviously that's also player-dependent... but even still, the four-player game always feels... off for some reason.  But only a tad.  This is still a wonderful design.

*********************************

Construir rutas de correo recolectando puntos de distintas formas (regiones, largo de rutas, presencia, etc). El mapa tiene los pueblos y las rutas entre ellos. Hay varias regiones (comunas) a lo largo del mapa En cada turno se roba una carta de pueblo, y luego se bota una carta de la mano en frente del jugador formando la ruta de correo. Las cartas colocadas deben respetar los caminos, por lo que si no puede ser extendida de forma legal se pierde la ruta. Si la ruta tiene al menos 3 cartas se puede declarar. Se debe decidir si poner las casas en cada pueblo de la ruta en 1 regi√≥n, o en 1 pueblo de cada regi√≥n.

RATING: 7
Light-to-medium weight networking and planning game, roughly the same game weight and game length as Web of Power. Not sure why people are comparing this to Ticket to Ride, other than the card draw. For some reason, the game makes me think of a kinder, gentler Amazonas. The reason being that TnT is a networking game with specific targets and bonuses that drive the game. It's less nasty than Amazonas in that you can't block people from entering a town entirely (unless you grab the cards for a specific city) and has no income management component to worry about. It plays well with 2. I have some minor concerns regarding the granularity of the scoring with 4 as the scoring can be very tight. Also not sure whether the 1pt bonus for ending the game is fair/necessary as it would seem to reward those earlier in the turn order and ending the game as early as possible is something you should be aiming for anyway. A tentative 7.5 after 10 plays on BSW.

RATING: 8

RATING: 10
[b]Spiel des Jahres 2006[/b]
[COLOR=#959595][SIZE=7]Deutscher Spiele Preis 2nd Place 2006[/SIZE][/COLOR]

[i]Posted: 12/23/07, Rated: 10[/i]

I've got a ticket to ride the Pony Express.

I have to admit that I suffered from a bit of [i]Thurn & Taxis[/i] backlash last year: there had been so much written about it, and my first attempts to learn it on BSW were less than stellar.  The theme of creating postal routes felt forced and uninteresting.  Role selection has become a common component of Euro games, so it didn't feel novel this time around.  Still, I collect games which have won the [i]Spiel des Jarhes.[/i]  I dutifully picked up a copy.

[i]Thurn & Taxis[/i] finally made it to the table.  And WOW!  Giddyup, horsies!  It really is an awesome game.

First, I think the board is gorgeous.  The color palette is wonderfully subdued, the artwork exquisite with pictures of actual historical buildings.  A page naming each building is included, and it is a nice touch.  I probably butcher the names of most of the towns while playing since they're all German, but the combination of these names plus the board really captures an "old world" feel.  The cards are small like those found in [i][gameid=9209][/i], well drawn and richly colored.

[i]Thurn & Taxis[/i] bears more than a passing resemblance to [i][gameid=9209][/i] in other areas as well.  You are attempting to complete a route by collecting a set of cards.  There are face-up cards on the table from which you can select, or you can draw "blind" from the deck.  Unlike [i][gameid=9209][/i], the routes aren't laid out for you.  There are roads between towns, but the route you are trying to construct can meander however you see fit within certain limitations.  The method of laying out your cards is a novel concept.  Each round, you must add to the "route" you are creating, by placing a card on either end of the set of cards you already have in play.  In this manner, you extend your route -- but you are also limited in which cities you can use to do so.  If you don't have any cards which represent cities connected to the cards on the end of your route, then you have to scrap your route and start over.  This can be a severe setback.  You have to adjust your route selection and card draws as appropriate.

Since you can't be locked out of a particular link, some of the "push your luck" from [i][gameid=9209][/i] is missing: you're never stuck waiting for your turn to come around, hoping that no one steals the link from Las Vegas to Los Angeles.  This removes some of the tension of [i][gameid=9209][/i], and resulted in a more social experience at the table.  There are also several different ways to accumulate points in [i]Thurn & Taxis[/i] which ultimately means different ways to win.  I feel as though [i][gameid=9209][/i] has been "solved" as a game: get your big east-west track laid, connect to several cities in the East, then raid the Destinations deck towards the end of the game.  Scores in [i]Thurn & Taxis[/i] end up being closer together, so this feeling of being "solved" has yet to set in for me.

The implementation of roles isn't new, and the "powers" these roles impart are pretty straightforward.  They essentially grant you an extra action each turn: you can draw an extra card, you can play an extra card, you can refresh all of the face-up cards (an excellent option if you're looking at the possibility of discarding your current route), or else "closing" and scoring a route with some help to make it an appropriate length.  These roles take what would normally be a boring set collection game, and effectively add that "wish you could do just one more thing" angst.  You can take two cards, but then you'll only be able to play one.  You could only take one card and then play two, but then you'll still be one short before you'll be able to score the route.  These considerations also provide an extra layer of strategy: how quickly can you gain the cards for a route, play them in sequence, and yet carry the maximum number of cards (three) into your next turn?  Careful role selection can help you to find the proper balance.

[i]Thurn & Taxis[/i] is a more open route creation game, removing some of the tension and conflict from [i][gameid=9209][/i], while also providing multiple paths to victory.  Neither snow, nor rain, nor heat, nor gloom of night stays this game from swiftly being a triumph.

RATING: 7
Last played with 3 players which works excellent. The game is not heavy and does not take too long to learn. The goal is to create the best postal service in old germany from what i understand and players compete in making routes through the counties. The player first to win a county will be awarded most points and the next player to finish that county will be awarded 2nd most points. To add to the twist you get points for being the first to make a longer route aswell as the first to have run a route and placed a postal office in each county. 
The first time you play it's pretty hard to grasp which tactics are the best and everything is decided by where your route can take you next. The key is to know when to end it as the player who first makes a route through 7 cities will end the game during that last turn. If you are in the lead it might be a good idea to worry about getting the long routes before competition can catch up. 

All in all a fun game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
It probably can be found at the heart of the Elfenland, Kardinal & Koenig and Atlantic Star triangle, with the player interaction seriously lost in the Bermuda fog.

Don't get me wrong, it is a wonderful game, looks pretty, plays quick, doesn't burn the brain, keeps everyone reasonably involved through various balances, maybe favours people who are offered the right card mix at the start of their turn but not so much that it will turn others off. A great filler, a perfect family game that fully deserves its Spiel des Jahres prize.

I'm taken in, but certainly not bowled over. If it had a wee bit more player interaction, especially a more effective way to make a leader's position a bit harder than burning their target cards and pray that the replacement isn't any better, I am certain I would like this a lot more. The prominence that the four postal officials have been given on the board certainly suggest that they had a slightly different allocation mechanic than the one present now. It so looks like a space where the game can show which role is assigned to which player.

Burning cards to get a better pick-up pool is seriously expensive, as you are starting to run 1 card behind everyone else's hand pools, and probably give the person following you 2 good cards in the process. If you compare it to how easy it is to complete a route with 2 cities missing, and what the rewards are if that strategy gets to its 7th route, then the burning ability is probably not worth using at all. Winners will be found amongst those who execute "pick-up 2, play 2" to maximum effect. "Complete route early"-strategies are devastating when they aren't countered by "over-build"-strategies that aim for maximum route length bonuses and house placements from the word go. A mixture of the strategies always seem to fall short by a turn or two. Don't build in the area where the person on your left is also active if you can avoid it. 

There are enough ways to win, or try to, to keep me interested, and the luck of the draw guarantees that all players will face different ebb and flood moments. But a player who gets the key cards at the right moment will be hard to beat. And the more people play, the harder the last player needs to work to keep up, as that player needs to get the right cards for the opening that is left, whereas earlier players can decide how to commit themselves based on the cards available, since the higher points are available from more places on the board. Best played with less people than the maximum 4.

We were wondering if their wasn't a variation possible that would allocate a special power to a player (the engineer role swapping of Canal Mania spring to mind) would make it (even) better?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very well-designed strategy game.  The diversity of the strategies make it quite enjoyable.  It is very light on the player interaction, but I don't mind that for my family.  The different routes to victory add a nice feel to the game, and will keep me coming back to learn more about this game.  The theme is not very deep, but the mechanics are sound without it.  I like how there are moments where you almost feel like you're playing a push-your-luck game as you take a risk without a playable city card, just hoping the right one will come up.

As I've played the game more I just keep getting more intrigued.  Every time it seems like the winner took a slightly different path.  Sometimes diversity is the key, and other times specialization works much better.  The scores are generally pretty close, which makes the endgame exciting.  My biggest complaint would probably be the tiebreaker.  It seems so arbitrary, and we've had at least 3 games end in a tie.  Aside from that this is a delightful game, that is just one step of complexity higher than another route building game...Ticket to Ride.

RATING: 7
So many people love this game so much that I expected a little more from it. But it was fine, and I'd play it again. There are many route games I prefer. My main complaint is that it's mostly like 4-player solitaire. You try to get certain bonuses before other people, but you don't really have enough control to make a huge impact on their plans. The only tense part is at the very end, trying to determine if someone's going to end the game before you have a chance to score your current route; it can force you to score it before you really need to.

NOTE: I had a better impression the second time I played it, but I still can't figure out why it's as popular as it is. However, I might consider buying it if the price were right. I can think of a few situations in which something of this weight would come in handy.

RATING: 6
Easy rules & fast playing game of set collection.  Luck of the draw is high, making this a tactical game with some potential for strategic play (e.g. saving cards for a long route).  There is a race aspect to scoring points; the first person to fill a region gets X points, 2nd to fill region gets X-1, and so on.  The variety of options to score points should keep this game interesting for repeated plays; however, I think the points system is structured such that final scoring will be tight, and players will be forced to build routes in a manner that will get them the most carriage points.

RATING: 7
I knew from reading the rules this game would be simple, yet one of those few games interesting for both the gamer and non-gamer. First two plays confirmed my impressions. Has just enough choices to make it interesting, with only a draw and play per turn unless scoring, and only 4 special actions from which players can select each turn. The forced discard of route cards is forgiving, allowing for players to stay in the game if they don't find the right route cards. Does not feel like TtR to me - the only similarity is drafting cards but the route laying is completely different.

4/18/06 - 10+ games. Came with Die Macher order from Germany. Translated the rules - thankfully they were only 4 pages. This game played out very well and the couple I played with really liked it. Some are saying this game has no interaction - I somewhat disagree because using the Administrator could totally mess up your opponents plan if you see there could be some city cards they could use for their route. Some tension exists in this game when you are building a route but have no cards to add to it AND there are no useful cards showing in the expense. This is good game to have as part of your gaming fun and it probably is an easy enough game to get non-gamers to play. Simple enough ruleset but contains some interesting and sometimes tension filled choices - but not a brain burner.

Update: 5/7/06 - this game scales very well.

RATING: 7
Sort of a "gamer's" version of Ticket to Ride or Elfenland. A route-building game with more texture and more subtley than these games, but it also is a little less accessible - lots of different, conflicting ways to score points means more confusion for the casual gamer. For reference, I'd play Ticket to Ride with my family, but Thurn und Taxis would probably be a tougher sell.

As a gamer, it's hard to give Thurn and Taxis a terribly low rating, because it's competantly put-together out of proven parts, and for me is a lot more appealing than Ticket to Ride. On the other hand, it's also hard to give it a high rating, for the same reasons: it's not so different from stuff we're already familiar with, the real player interaction is a little too sparse, there can be an aggravating lack of control, and the theme is weak. It's never going to compete with Beowulf, Blue Moon City, Settlers, or El Grande for raw fun factor because the other players in the game feel too distant.

Still, I liked Thurn and Taxis. But to be honest, I liked Rum and Pirates more, and Blue Moon City (against which it is up for the Spiel des Jahre) is the vastly cleaner, more interesting, more interactive, and more fun game.

Addenda: Thurn und Taxis won the Spiel des Jahre. A conservative, and ultimately rather disappointing pick. Blue Moon City was, to my mind, the unquestionably superior game. Blue Moon City will still be hitting the table in a couple years. Thurn und Taxis will not.

RATING: 6.5
A solid medium-weight game featuring many German design virtues. As it is clearly shooting for the family-oriented Spiel des Jahres award, the screwage/confrontational/player interaction element happens to be characteristically low, which gamers such as me will find somewhat lacking.

Player interaction is limited to the card management aspect, the race to acquire certain scoring tiles and the like. Unlike in say Zug um Zug you cannot block routes by first building them.

This has led me to devise following (4-player) variant, which seeks to increase player interaction and confrontation:

You may only build a house in a town already containing one, if posessing the 3 level coach. The same goes for towns containing two houses in respect to the 4 level coach and three houses in a town and the 5 level coach card. Note you may still have a route leading through such towns. Merely house/post station placement will be prevented unless you've reached the corresponding coach level.

I recommend a somewhat different scheme for less than four players.

In any case TuT is a thoroughly enjoyable medium-weight game with very atmospheric Biedermeier-style artwork. Together with the above-described variant rules, TuT may even be an 8 for me.

I'm most confident that it'll at least be nominated for the SdJ and might well even win the great prize (though I have to admit I'm not well acquainted with other likely candidates).

RATING: 5.8
So, shoot me, I don't think the new SDJ winner is all that great.  I told people I thought it was good, but that was based on BSW plays, a fitting format for this type of game.  Over the board, if you are not playing this game with two or maybe 3 fast players, this game is dreadfully boring and long, while lacking really all that much complexity.  For some reason, people think this game takes a whole lot of thought.  I maybe make 3-4 decisions a game that take a total of 90 seconds.  Nobody follows my lead.  People with any tendency toward AP will destroy this game.  

Furthermore, there isn't any confrontation to this tactical game.  Now, nobody will mistake me for someone who begs for confrontation in a game, but if you have a totally tactical game, for example, Hacienda, China, Hansa, Age of Steam, you will need non-screwage confrontation to keep the game from totally sucking ass.  Also, there is no balance for turn order.  If you are playing with 3-4 players, the person in the last seat gets jacked.  They are a turn behind to get bonus tiles.  Equalizing turns doesn't help, unless you have perfect timing.  You could end up having a last turn where you can do nothing at all.

When it comes to this game, you might as well be playing with yourself.  At least you won't be bored, nobody will get in your way, and you will be done in no time.

RATING: 7.5
I like this game.  It is a well done set-collection type of game similar in some ways to [gameid=9209].  A decent amount of depth and strategy, with only of touch of opponent "screwage" if you can take a card that you know someone else needs.  Overall, a well done game, and worthy of its Spiel des Jahres award.

Oh, and don't be fooled by the theme - or what is in actuality a pretensious attempt at one.  This game could be played with a blank board with lines between lettered boxes, and a collection of blank cards with nothing but those self-same letters, and it would be exactly the same.  The theme does not make this game what it is.  That alone makes it clearly a Euro in the strongest sense.  If [gameid=181] and [gameid=103] are your favorite games, this is probably NOT for you.  If you like [gameid=9209] or other set-collection type games, then this will do quite fine.  This game single-handedly defines "Multiplayer Solitaire".  Despite all of that, however, it is still a very fun game.

RATING: 7
I really like this one.  I just thought it was fun and interesting.  It's true that it's a cross between Ticket to Ride (really even the Marklin edition) and Web of Power.  It packs a punch for the time it takes to play (45 minutes).  There's lots of tension between trying to finish off routes fast to get tiles you want, but then also finishing them off slowly to get points for longer routes.  You have to hustle to keep up with the impending game's end.  The game is not too hard to end, so you have to watch out.  Very fun.  Plays reasonably well 2-4, maybe best with 3.  One tiny gripe: you put your houses on the cities and then you can't read the names of them so well, but no big deal.

RATING: 8
Let me start by saying that I really dislike Ticket to Ride. Now this game is nothing like that other game, but it often gets compared to it.

Now, I really like this game. Instead of just building routes and blocking, you have many more options on where to build your post offices on the route. You then compete over resources that vary based on those decisions. 

To me this is a much deeper game than Ticket to Ride, and quite enjoyable. 

This is still rather new to me, so my opinion might change after a few plays.

RATING: 5
Perfectly acceptable welterweight Euro, to such an extent that it seems like a parody of the form -- between its rigorously dorky subject matter, its Germany-of-the-vaguely-indeterminate-olden-days setting, its inscrutable-to-Americans title, and its simple (but not lobotomized) level of difficulty, it's maybe the [i]Euroest[/i] Eurogame I've played. For all that, it's not bad at all and I would happily play again.

RATING: 6.5
I've got no problem with the theme. I find it kind of cute.  The board is beautiful, and reminded me of Hansa....maybe the color scheme. The card-drafting has been used multiple places before, most well-known is probably Ticket to Ride. The comparisons are unavoidable. 

While the "role selection" is a fun piece to consider each turn, and helps with card management.....it left me wanting more. The biggest criticism with the game is the limited player interaction. That doesn't bother me a whole lot, because its still there. The diminishing points values for items that are being contested seems like a pretty direct interaction, but maybe its less obvious where that occurs to some players. But I kind of wanted the role selection to impact each player more. 

Overall, Thurn und Taxis was a bit on the dry side (not a negative to me), visually appealing, with nice decisions to make. Its short enough that the weaknesses aren't an issue. There are plenty of other games that provide a similar feel to this one, but I like it better than Ticket to Ride...for the obvious comparison. I fear the theme will keep it generally behind TtR in the public perception, though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Uhh...it's better than Ticket to Ride, but that's like saying poop is better than crap as far as I'm concerned.  Seriously folks, most of us have played Ticket To Ride and Web of Power so why is this the "hot" game of the minute?  There is literally NOTHING in this game that either of those games doesn't provide in terms of (_extremely_ limited) decision making, route planning, card drafting, set collecting, and area control.  I _never_ go into a game expecting something completely, brilliantly original- in fact, I think building on established game concepts and design ideas is healthy and interesting- but this game is arguably even less original than last year's Vegas Showdown.  I really expected more from Mr. Seyfarth, and his only contribution to the T2R/Web of Power chassis is a variation on the role selection thing that we've already seen of course in Puerto Rico.  TuT represents a phase that I think the Euro design paradigm is going through right now where it's completely stagnant and it's cannibalizing itself, cranking out games that are fundamentally the same 3 or 4 archetypes over and over again to appease this core audience of gamers that will still buy anything with wooden pieces or a Hans Im Gluck logo on it.  As far as the themeing goes, here's an anecdote.  Another player failed to complete a route toward the end of the game and someone said "I guess the mail didn't get through".  I came to a realization at that point that not once during the game did the thought of planning a postal route, establishing offices, or any of that even enter my mind.  I was thinking about cards, pieces, and a board as well as exploiting the system to the best possible effect.  The whole thing could literally have been plain white cards with plain, black font city names played on a plain, white board with plain, black font and it literally wouldn't have made a difference to me.  In fact, I think it would have been more honest than wrapping up a completely generic, hackneyed game in a beautifully illustrated package with a mail delivery theme.  Maybe I'm spoiled by "Snakes on a Plane", which tells you up front what it is without any folderol.  Does the game work?  Of course it does, mechanically it's like an oiled machine- why shouldn't it be?   There is some weirdness in regards to the scoring- I was losing the whole game and wound up literally blowing everyone away with a single route in the very last turn of the game that netted me twice the points I had going into it.  But hey, what do I know...I'm a heretic, a hater, and all those other things that people have called me for trashing the currently popular Eurogames.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Thurn und Taxis I played twice to date. Once was during Spiel 2006, when it had just been crowned as SdJ-winner. My girlfriend wanted to play this game (she wants to test most SdJ-winners), and so we sat down, and wondered about an hour later where the appeal lay. Direct interaction is fairly low, it all seemed a matter of grabbing points, so... what?

Forward to the second time I played. I entered the game with an open mind as I would be playing a family member's copy he had shown us very proudly, and I didn't want to ruin the good mood‚Äîin addition, it [i]had[/i] been a long time, and maybe my view on matters had changed. And indeed it had. The interaction is rather subtle in this game: there is pressure to not get stuck with too many malus points in the form of unbuilt houses, and once most larger bonuses have been taken the race is on for the remainder. Viewed like that TuT was definitely better than I remembered. However, the game suffers from a few serious buts. There is, of course, the luck of the draw. The game is not particularly keen on players hoarding cards as you must discard down to three upon completing a route. This makes matters more ad hoc than I like. Second is that near the end the game begins to drag; most options are closed, players are looking for very specific cards, and matters turn into a boring race. Third is that the current ruleset does not really allow for a larger difference in the amount of cards drawn and played. You must draw one and play one, augmented by either drawing or playing an extra. The average net effect cancels out the extras, once again strengthening the luck of the draw.

All in all I think the game is better than I remembered, but not by much. It is very unfortunate the fluid feeling of the first few rounds appears to be unmaintainable, otherwise I'd probably like the game even more. With the current ruleset I get the feeling that the players are not really allowed a lot of strategical space to explore, and that is, I think, a bit of a shame. Still, I'm increasing the rating, the old note was too harsh.

RATING: 8
I read that one of the drawbacks to the game was a lack of player interaction, but I didn't see it as such. Just because you are not doing something directly to your opponent doesn't mean that his choices do not affect yours (and vice versa); and part of an effective strategy can be to keep your opponent in check (denying a card or grabbing a scoring marker that he was aiming for). So where is the lack of interactrion?

I also see randomness in the cards as being offset by having six cards to choose from, drawing blind, or resetting the available cards. This, along with good hand management and a little forethought, practically negates the randomness factor. 

With those two complaints put to rest after only one play, the game as a whole was solid and thoughtful, though lacking an interesting theme for me.

Players build post office routes across Bavaria and the regions around, collecting bonus points in various ways.
Players build postal routes from city to city to city so that each city is adjacent to the next city on the route and there is a road connecting these two cities. Each route must consist of at least three cities. Players may only build one route at a time. Routes are represented by melded city cards arranged in the order of the route. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Good game here. It has a short set of rules that is relatively quick to learn and to teach (I say "relatively", because I did look up a couple rules questions that had already been asked and answered on The Geek). Players must create routes to build a postal system by playing city cards that are drawn at the beginning of the their turn (and/or from their hand). Card routes are layed out on the table in a horizontal line and cards for adjacent cities can be placed on either end of the line. When the player has at least three cards in a route, they can opt to "cash in" the route or keep it on the table to be further expanded in the coming turns (only one route per player is allowed on the table at a time). When a route is cashed in, the player can place post offices either in every city in one region or one city in each different region that the route moves through. The player can score victory point tiles for having post offices in all cities in a region (or all cities in pre-specified regional pairs) and if the player has presence in all regions (save the largest) this also nets a point tile. If the player's route included 5 or more cities, they pick up a route length VP tile. "Carriage Cards" are also available for route sizes 3-7 and they provide VPs when obtained. These Carriage Cards must be obtained in tiered steps (i.e.- the player cannot get the "7" length card before getting the "6"). There are four different Postal Officials, each with a different power that can help the player out, but only one can be utilized per turn. Most of the postal duties help with hand management, but the Cartwright worker lets the player grab a higher tiered Carriage card than the route warrants. All of this makes for a fun and light-hearted game. There aren't really that many excrutiating decisions to be made and I think most eurogame buffs would be more than willing to give this game a try. It's not a "postal Ticket to Ride", but I do feel that TtR fans would probably also like this game. I absolutely love the period feel provided by Michael Menzel's beautiful artwork and the historical overview provided. Thurn and Taxis won the 2006 Spiel des Jahres award and I think it was a well-deserved win. Recommended!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Hand management game with zip player interaction. The hand management is fun and thoughtful, but we've seen it before.

Also depressingly tactical in that, apart from planning an extended route and hoping you get the cards, you can't count on much forward strategy. The cards you see now will almost certainly be gone by the time its your turn. And anyway, you can always sweep them all away yourself.

Enjoyable enough, but hasn't really grabbed me. I think its because we've seen it all before and the special abilities seem to soften the hash edge of other card drafting games rather than add depth. eg, compare being able to draw extra cards or play extra cards (and you do both!) compared to TtR's "you can only draft OR play". Or the angst when a TtR extra locomotive comes up and resets the draft options just when the card you wanted comes up where in T&T you can CHOOSE when to sweep or not. It all becomes too easy, relaxed and lacks tension and excitement.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
True, player interaction is minimal: basically, you pay attention to which bonus chits your opponents have taken or are about to take and to whether someone is about to trigger the end game. The most common comparison is to Ticket to Ride, but the game reminds me more of Elfenland in the need to have the right card for each path and to visit all or nearly all of the cities.

RATING: 7.5
This is the good version of Ticket to Ride. Properly fixes card hoarding and long-route runs that fundamentally break Ticket to Ride. Still falls short on a really immersive theme, and while I don't believe the ability to claim routes in Ticket to Ride is an actual competitive mechanic, this game still doesn't have routes perfect (nobody should end a route early to gain the +1 advantage in tile scoring at the cost of being an entire carriage-length behind). Still, if you want a game with average luck but lots of critical decision making, this is pretty good.

RATING: 7
Players are founding postal routes around Barvaria, and it's neighbouring provinces.  Players on their turn draft a city card, then build a city card into their current route.  They can optionally close and score a route, which perhaps qualifies them for various victory points sprinkled around the board.  A solid Alan-Moon like drafting/route planning game, beautifully presented, and typically developed to a knife edge by Hans im Glueck.  The look and feel of the game play may just get me past the fact we've seen it all before, several times over.  Recommended.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A game based on the origins of the postal system in Europe.  Players try to build postal routes that span the different regions of 17th century Bavaria.  Points are awarded for length of route, for covering all cities in a region, or for establishing routes across all regions.

RATING: 4
This game came highly recommended to me and has won some prestigious rewards, so I put it on my Christmas wish list.  One of the selling points to me was the different theme from any of the games I already owned.  Once I got the game I was a little disappointed.  The mail rout theme was fresh, but the game play was mediocre.  The game simply did not get my brain working very hard.  The decisions of what cards to pick up are rarely challenging, and you often have a pretty good idea of what mail routes your opponents are working on.  The competition is quite indirect, which suits some, but not me.  Its not a bad game, but I would much rather play something more engaging.

I recently auctioned this item off in the Jack Vassel auction.  My thoughts and prayers go out to the whole Vassel family.  I'm happy to have been able to help out in a small way by donating this game.

RATING: 6

People who like a more confrontational game would probably prefer Ticket to Ride whereas T&T maybe be considered a bit more multi-player solitaire when compared to TtR.  The reason I prefer T&T is that there are multiple ways to end the game (one of which you have a lot of contol over), and in T&T I feel like I'm doing something every turn, whereas in TtR there are many turns I'm just going to be picking up train cards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A really good network-building game. Gameplay is similar to Ticket to Ride, however unlike Ticket to Ride, players cannot block routes from each other. The goal of Thurns and Taxis however is not to make singular routes, but rather to try to cover as much of the board as quickly as possible. Players can pick up victory points in lots of different ways, depending on what strategies and priorities they make for themselves. My favorite aspect of this game is that there is no single strategy that will guarantee victory, as players will inherent lose points from certain goals as the pick up points for gaining other goals.

RATING: 7.5
My group and I have a soft spot for this game. With experienced players, getting the cards you want becomes much harder. Lodz usually gets taken even if a player is not working that route at the moment. The challenge is in managing what you need to do without ever having to spend your bonus moves flushing the tableau. When nobody is taking one for the team and refreshing the cards, it's a tense race with a bit of brinksmanship. While waiting for cards to add to your carriage is tempting, you may be better cutting this route short and starting a new route with the cards you've built up.

RATING: 7
Often compared to Ticket to Ride, this game is very enjoyable, though perhaps not quite so enjoyable as the other. You do not compete to place routes, but to claim goals for servicing certain combinations of cities, which can be claimed only by having the right cards, much like Big City or Acquire. Fortunately there is more than one card for each city so it's not quite as cutthroat as it would be otherwise.

RATING: 7.3
Thurn and Taxis is a simple, basic worker-placement, route-creating game about building a postal empire in old Bavaria. It offers little new to the genre, but still remains a really solid game. What I really like about it is that it is simple and easy to play, while still providing enough subtle strategy options to keep it interesting. What you have here is an A+ gateway game to Eurogames for beginners that won't bore the expert to death. Nice.

RATING: 8
I think it's unfair to compare this game to Ticket to Ride.  There is no player competition for routes and your routes don't connect.  It's a game about getting your houses on the board in as efficient a way as possible (they are negative points if not placed at game end).  You build city routes as a means of delivering the houses to the board, and your choice of routes lets you 'aim' their placement.  Beyond that you can create strategies to pick up lots of bonus tiles, speed up the game timer, or react to other players strategies.  The game is full of options.  It's very easy to get to the table and both my family and my gaming group are into it.  It's a good two player game as well.  Our first play was slow as we were learning the map but after that it speeds up quite a bit.  This game fits in my favorite slot of under and hour playing time and medium weight.

RATING: 6
Thurn and Taxis is yet another set collection card game for 2 to 4 players whose theme (German postal service?) is completely detached from its mechanics.

The game itself does have depth and requires a certain degree of skill, as the luck of the draw is mitigated well by the board (which contains 6 face up cards) and the postmaster (that allows you to draw two cards).
I actually find it enjoyable. My problem with it is that there are already so many set collection card games with a pasted up theme. For me these games really don't feel that great without a good and possibly well integrated theme. If I weren't winning so much, I think I'd play [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/71836/onirim]Onirim[/url] over this.

RATING: 7.5
After ten years of playing Euros, I finally played this title for the first time, and it was a pleasant surprise.  It's the kind of game that would be produced if China and Ticket to Ride had a baby.  There's some interesting, albeit somewhat luck-influenced route-planning and building, along with some tense area majority strategy to deal with.  Knowing where each city is on the board is critical, and I wish it was easier to identify where on the map the city on each card is (it's just words and shared colors as printed), but it's not a hard game to learn if it's explained right.

RATING: 7
The theme is quite terrible - typical Euro and so it's a little fun to hate the theme - but egads! There is no way to interest someone in this game through theme-first tactics. It has a TtR feel as everyone says, but I like this one slightly more than TtR. More thought into your moves and less screwage on your opponents is what a relatively simple route collection should be. At the end of the day, there's lots of solid planning that has to be done to win, but T&T gets a bit boring if it goes on too long as it is a rather routine game. Take card, play card, score. At least players don't mull over their moves for too long in TtR; in T&T there's a bit of wait time while people plan. In a multiplayer solitaire sorta game, this can be a bit of a killjoy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
The more I play this the less I think of it.  It is most certainly very luck-dependent as you can get completely screwed by the cards available to you.  If you have to flush the field more than once in your game, you are probably behind the curve and it will be difficult to win.  And if you have to trash one of your routes before scoring, it's over for you.

But that's not the problem; I like lots of games that are very luck-dependent.  Thurn & Taxis' problem is that it doesn't have anything else going for it.  The feel of the gameplay is of a serious, thoughtful game, but it doesn't have the strategy to hold your interest.  There's no tension of the kind that makes Ticket to Ride so great.  And there's no player interaction because what you do rarely affects other players (yes, I know card-drawing affects other players, but come on, that's piddly).  Nor is there any excitement like there is in Beowulf's risks and showdowns.  

So now you've got a game that feels like a serious game (no excitement, no tension, no interaction) but is actually very luck-dependent.  That's a real loser combination.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
An intriguing little game, Thurn and Taxis encourages you to gather points while making it nearly impossible to do so.  Frequently you can spend all your efforts just trying to obtain one point bonus (it's all about the bonuses) just to find that someone else gets the bonus you were after and an extra one with apparently little effort.  While it's a fun game to try, it can be frustrating, when other people pick up the only cards you need, sometimes deliberately.  It is a tactical game, after all.  It's short (around 60-90 minutes, maximum).  Watch out for people who make frequent use of the carriage house dude.  It's a great way to gain bonuses quickly and finish the game earlier than when other people may be ready for it to end.  All in all, a good game.

RATING: 5.5
Another Seyfarth's game with a ridiculously boring theme. But this time there is the excuse of not making another train game - which in essence it is. I enjoyed it at first, as there seemed to be some interesting choices, but I found that they don't really matter much, and it seems that everyone who plays with at least reasonable competence will eventually get to close results. It's just a race for various bonuses, and a lot comes down to luck of the draw. It does feel a little like a heavier and slightly better "Ticket to Ride", but with less tension and player interaction, since all players can share all cities and routes. And I couldn't see the point in penalizing players with pieces left in the end of the game.

RATING: 6
A decent route building game with a strong element of time pressure in trying to get the high scoring tiles for an area before others, and driving the game to an end when you need it to end. The job of balancing what bonuses you would like to score against what cards are going to be most available given other people's routes is enjoyable but becomes a little obvious on repeated plays. I feel the key to winning though is forcing the game to end at the right time for you, and the carriage race is the most tense and exciting part of the game for me.

Traded away in a job lot, in which I got Hansa Teutonica in return. I cannot imagine why I would play T&T instead of HT anymore.

RATING: 8
I was afraid that it would Thurn bland and lifeless.
I particularly like the fact that the "route" each player is trying to build is visible, and the character you choose each turn for that extra edge.
I also realised, after my two or three first games, that there were strategy and planning in there: it is not a brain burner, but an experienced player will kick a newbie ass everytime, lucky or not.
Building a postal system for Germany seemed a bit dull as a theme but it turns out to be rather interesting, thanks to the spotless HiG design and bits, and also to the interesting history booklet in the box.
A strong and enjoyable game.

Update: this is simply turning into one of my favourite games, I just cannot wait till my next session.

RATING: 4
Thurn and Taxis is a game of network building, timing, and variable actions. As Seyfarth's foray into family gaming after his hit Puerto Rico, we see some similarities in the random draws of locations as well as the variable powers that you can do on your turn. However, where this game takes a few cues from Ticket to Ride, it also does away with much of the interaction that either PR or TTR have: gone are blocking and agressive shipping and role selection. Accordingly, T&T is largely a race. This is not to say that it is a bad game, but there's not too much for me here to actively seek out additional plays other than if it is for a very quick and casual play. Unfortunately, if you happen to play with a group who overthinks every move, what should be a quick and playful romp turns into a real slog (as the game also isn't as straight forward and simple as TTR). In other words, the added complexity doesn't necessarily add much more in terms of depth.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I would suggest this game as a gateway game (comparable to Ticket to Ride in terms of complexity), so good for families and casual gamers.

RATING: 6
[Initial Impression: 7]
This game is impressively engineered: it's a well-oiled machine.  It is unfortunate that such simple  and integrated mechanics lead to such a routinized feeling during play.  Players gather sets of cards that they play to form routes, which allow them to place post offices in cities.  Players score points for completing routes of certain lengths and for placing sets of post offices. Players recruit various roles with special powers to help them on each turn.  The really clever bit is that the points scored for meeting each condition decrease if another player meets that same condition first. (This is very similar to the passenger scoring in Ticket to Ride: Marklin, but it doesn't diminish the cleverness.)  This turns it into basically a race to meet each condition first so that you'll score more points than your opponent.  You have to really carefully manage your route-planning (it also helps to get just a little lucky in your card draws) in order to stay competitive.  A few mistakes can really cost you the game. For the most part, each player is just building their own routes in isolation.  But competitive play does require interaction through A) grabbing bonus tiles before other players do and B) blocking maneuvers by keeping track of what cards other players need and trying to deny these to them (by taking them yourself or by clearing them off the board). The main problem for me was that even though some players catch on to the really competitive nature of the game more quickly than others, the scoring seems engineered to keep point counts reasonably close. Most games are won or lost by just a few points, but it isn't that thrilling once you realize that the closeness occurs because of the way the scoring mechanics work, not necessarily because you're well-matched with your opponents.

RATING: 6.5
I really wanted to like this game, and I did like it fairly well.  It has simple rules, fairly clever mechanics (I enjoyed being able to add onto my routes from either end.) and a variety of strategies.

Unfortunately it was shot down by the girlfriend for the same reasons that Hansa didn't work:
 - Not very confrontational.  (Perhaps it would be more interesting for her if we were competing for the same cities.)
 - In early plays, you seem to "Get points by accident".  She got 6 points and won because she accidentally had one post office in each region (color).
 - theme is weakly tied together.  The "post offices" look like houses, and I really didn't get the feeling that I was building a post office empire.  It is a little odd that you are building routes, but the routes themselves (the connections between two cities) is not represented on the board, rather only the cities themselves are represented.

It is a well thought out system and gameplay seems smooth, but it isn't exceptionally engaging.  (It is more engaging than Hansa.)  It feels somewhere between Power Grid and Ticket to Ride in terms of both complexity and engaging-ness.

RATING: 7
Thurn and Taxis fixes many of the route building issues that I saw in Ticket to Ride, and replaced them with a set of diminishing awards, permitting players to repeat routes if desired, but also rewarding the first players to do so.

It seems an inevitable comparison, but in mnay ways Thurn and Taxis is both more forgiving and more interesting to me than the Ticket to Ride series, especially when one considers the race to gain Carriages, the race to complete routes, and the race to venture to all the postal regions.

Some of the gaminess of the various route cards is still here, but on the whole I think I'd prefer a game of Thurn and Taxis to Ticket most days.

RATING: 8
Simple mechanics, straight-forward play, with oodles of fun and thought provoking options, that plays in 60 minutes--What is there not to like? I can see why this won the Spiel Des Jahres, as it is accessible to both young and old alike, and could serve as an excellent gateway game. Relatively small decision tree, with a card selection mechanic a la Ticket To Ride, with a push-your-luck element in whether to continue extending your network or cash-in and take those VPs. Only quibble is that there is no player interaction, except for how the card queue is manipulated by the players. Otherwise, it is a traveling salesman optimization VP race game, but it scratches the itch for something between a filler and a medium-weight game.

Update (1/2/11) 4 plays: Quickly dropping out of sight as quickly as it rose; it has no staying power as it is essentially multi-player solitaire, and who wants that? Not me.

RATING: 8
First played this on Jan 24, 2007.  An interesting easy game to play. The rules seem a bit difficult to grasp, however after a few turns it starts to come together.  There is a decent amount of strategy to this and is different enough from other games to warrent a position in the rotation of games to play. Each player starts with 20 houses. The board starts with 6 city cards to choose from and victory points placed on the board. You build mail routes to score. On your turn you MUST take a city card, you MUST play a city card into a new or existing mail route. That card must be adjacent to the previous city(ies) or you will need to start a new mail route (wiping previous). You may go through a city multiple times. You MAY score your mail route. To score, you must have at least 3 cities. You may place houses in either all of the same color OR 1 in each different color card city. After placement, you check to see if you recieve any bonus VP for havhing a long route or all of a region. Also when you complete a route, you upgrade your carriage house for VP if you have enough cities played to match the carriage house you are getting. You must take the carriage house upgrades in order, 3,4,5,6,7 without skipping. Once during each turn you MAY use an official to help out your cause: Postmaster (place two cities); P (gain two cities); Administrator (clear and replace 6 city cards to choose from); X (upgrade carriage house 1 or 2 more than cards played).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Thurn & Taxis. Wow. This game blew me away. At the onset, the rules set seems dry, particularly the system of how bonus points tiles are awarded. However, I loved the mental puzzle of trying to optimize the play of my cards in establishing postal routes in 16th century Germany, Austria, and surrounding areas. I like the weight and length of the game: a two hour game with relatively simple rules set--not Knizia simple, but significantly less complex than Five Tribes or Concordia. This game spoke to me and I enjoyed it immensely. It really surprised me. I wasn't expecting a lot and T & T is very fun. I would liken it as a more interesting, deeper version of ticket to ride. I can't wait to play this game some more.

RATING: 8
I good gateway game.  While you collect cards  to create routes, you also try to place your pieces in the different cities and regions so you can get the bonus points.  You also want to create longer routes so you can get the better carriages.  A fun game with several things to work on. 

As has been mentioned in some reviews and sessions reports you do not want to think about what cards you will take next in a 3 or 4 player game. If you try  to plan to far ahead you will get frustrated as the available cards will change considerably before it is your turn again.  Wait until the player before you to start looking at the available cards.

RATING: 2.8
Got this is a trade mainly because it was favorably compared to Ticket to Ride.  It's a fine game, but it's not nearly as great as ticket to ride.

Pros: I really like the "push-your-luck" aspect of the route building.  I enjoy the risky decisions that it offers to me.  I also enjoy the decision of what "character" to enlist help from each turn.

Cons: It's a passive aggressive play land.  The only interaction is to hate draft the cards, and it's hard to do that without hurting yourself.  The biggest Con though, is that it's just not as good as TtR.  It takes longer to setup, it takes longer to explain, it doesn't have as nice of components, and it just isn't as elegant (Don't keep more than 3 cards?!?  Have to choose the take 2 guy?!?  Bugger that.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Thurn and Taxis is a beautifully presented game with simple rules and thus is eminently suitable for playing with non-gamers. It also offers something for dedicated boardgamers. On a tactical level, the game is more complex than it seems at first glance. Turn-by-turn decisions are important in terms of the efficiency of placing houses and managing the risk of the cards. Tactical mistakes (particularly towards the end of the game) can significantly prejudice a player's final score. The well-balanced scoring mechanisms further enhance the tactical possibilities and even add an element of longer term planning to the game. On the negative side, there is little interaction between players and a lack of variation means that the game starts to become stale after several plays.

RATING: 6
initial rating

It's true what most people are saying, its like TtR with a little more going on, very little interaction, themeless, a dose of luck, suspected bad replay value, it also ran a little long with 4 players. Several ways to score on the positive side with scoring depreciating the longer you take to do it but not being able to block was totally bad in a route planning game. Would like to try with 2 or 3 players and suspect with those numbers this might be better, also, this might be better suited for "quicker" online play. Its not 'bad' but I won't be requesting this...ever

Update: 5 => 6 Played again years later, MUCH better with only 2 or 3 players, not as long or as much downtime (it wasnt high but now it is less). It makes a decent filler with the scoring options as the highlight, but is still a touch long for that class otherwise my rating might have been 6.5 or 7. 

The luck is not bad at all actually especially for the games length and having the option to clear the board helps and was a nice touch as was having 6 cards face up. Wouldn't request this but would likely play again, found it to be much better than China also which I recently played (and of course worlds better than TtR).

Keep seeing words like 'Solid' and 'Good' on these comments and this sums Thurn up pretty well. It's... nice enough. Game of the Year? Maybe. Interesting fact : A non gamer friend actually told me the 'theme of the game seems to not quite fit' - interesting and arguably true. The theme is well done, unusual, and lavishly applied, but all a bit too thickly for my tastes. 
As he said; "What the hell has putting houses in coloured areas got to do with delivering mail?". We know the answer, of course, but it DOES feel a lot like that when playing.

Initial Rating : 6.5 (December 2011)

I had high hopes for Thurn and Taxis, lots of my friends liked the game a lot but after some plays it is a disappointment for me. I liked my first game, but with each play I rated it lower and lower. I feel all the games play the same way so I have no desire to further explore this.

I don¬¥t like anything about the game, the theme is awful and bland (to me), takes a long playtime for what the game offers, it has obvious decisions, when playing with 4 I feel a lot of downtime, the game is not fun and it has a lot of luck of the draw. 

On the plus side, the components are ok I guess, and this game could be used to introduce new gamers to the hobby as it is very light and family friendly, but it is not a game for me.

I only play it when a friend wants to and I always offer some resistance to the suggestion.

RATING: 5
After 1 play: Draw one or two cards from a face up pool of six, collect connected sets to create routes, play the sets one card at a time (with a "push your luck" aspect) until you decide to cash in your route. Race to be the first (or early) to achieve each of about ten different objectives... in many ways it [i]is[/i] reminiscent of Ticket to Ride. But with much [i]less[/i] player interaction. (Yup, you heard it folks ... even less interaction than TtR.)

I'm really not great with multiplayer solitaire. (And I don't often use this phrase about a game.)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
If you are expecting a heavy, brain burner of a game with T&T, you aren't going to get it.  The game plays light and fast.

Player interaction is all in the race to get the 'Bonus Chips' which are worth Victory Points.   Even though there is only a 1 or 2 point difference in the chip values, because of the tight end-game scoring, EVERY SINGLE POINT can make a BIG difference.

Only six of the 22 areas will, more than likely, be represented in the face up cards, so clearing the board will normally show less than half the areas you may want to build.  Whoever said that there is no player interaction has never been screwed out of a card they've had their eye on by the player to their right as they discard the six face-up cards, knowing full well that you needed one of them. "Didn't get a replacement?  Oops, sorry!"... "Yeah, I'll bet you are."

It also has a bit of a "push your luck" element, when attempting to build a route without a card in hand.  Seeing six-length route disappear because you fail to play a card can be very demoralising... especially if getting the right card would have put you in the lead.

T&T has just the right mix of skill and luck, with enough subtle play to keep most gamers interested.  It is definitely worthy of its Spiel des Jahres win.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A fun and (once you've learned it) quick game of building postal routes through Germany.  Anyone who's read "The Crying of Lot 49" must play this game at least once!  Every round you draw and play to a route, then optionally cash the route in.  Nice role mechanic lets you decide between drawing twice, playing twice, or doing various "I'm screwed" mechanics.  Nice eschaton mechanic makes it critical that you build up about as quickly as your opponents, and can make it tricky to decide between a long and high-scoring route and a short route that will move you forward more quickly (and allow you to place more of your little birdhouses on the board).

This is no PR, or Caylus, or Settlers, but it is quite a good little game in its own right and the quick play time makes it easy to squeeze in before or after a longer game.  The downside: too easy to pull it out when we're too lazy to play something better but longer!

RATING: 7.5
I can see where some might compare it to Web of Power or China.  It has a similar feel regarding weight and having the cards dictate your possibilities.  For me, this is better than Web of Power, and way better than China (don't get me started on that one).  I like the many options available for scoring, I like the special actions (or whatever they're called), I like the decision between placing 1 PO in each country vs. all of 1 country, and I like the extend your route or wipe it clean mechanic. All in all, about the worst thing I can say about it is the theme is weird and the tie-breaker is lame (the person who ended it or clockwise from the person who ended it).

RATING: 6.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A route-connecting game with a charming theme. Players are competing couriers in the 16th century mail service in Germany, racing to establish long-rang service, intra-province service, and inter-province service. It works well enough and held an 8 rating from me for 10-12 plays. I just don't see much in it now. The game really does seem played-out, and the only thing left is for players to become good card-counters. It's well designed and beautifully produced - no question, but the strategies (are there more than two or three?) all play out in roughly the same way once you're familiar with the game. Essentially, it's not that hard to make your decisions, which foregrounds the random draw element (only partly addressed by game mechanics). Sad to say, but currently this is more like a six for me. Perhaps a twice-a-year game. Only keeping it because it's popular with the SO. That, and I'm fond of the quirky theme. 

Traded away April 2009.

RATING: 7
The more I play this game, the less it appeals to me. It's certainly a good game, and I do enjoy it but the desire to play it has faded considerably since it came out. I strongly believe this game should not have won the SDJ and instead Blue Moon City should have won... but what are you going to do...

The mechanics in this game are very solid and it's easy enough to explain. There isn't much in the way of differing strategies. You want to build lots of routes and you would like to get chits with lots of points, so it's best to be the first one to get a post office in each city of a district. This game is really about hand management and a bit of risk assessment since you might need to hope for a card to play when you do the administration action on your next turn. 

I guess the real downside to the game is that there isn't anything there for the experienced player, the first 4 to 6 games will be very fun and rewarding as you learn how to play the game, but there isn't anything waiting for you on the other side of that learning curve.

RATING: 7
A pretty good light-middleweight Euro. Certainly it has some similarities to Ticket to Ride, but it also has similarities with area-control/-influence games. You are, of course, connecting routes, but you don't foreclose other people's routes by making your own, nor are you (as is effectively the case in TtR) restricted from going into the same city twice. Indeed, I think if you play it like TtR, you're likely to miss the fact that it's not just about connecting cities but about enclosing areas.

RATING: 7
I like this game. The rules are simple yet there is some depth to the game play. Timing is crucial and sometimes the key play can be to take a shorter route than necessary for the next carriage card in order to get the higher bonus tile in a particular province. I think this game is different enough from Ticket to Ride that you could find room on your shelf for both, though I think this one wins out in my opinion. The game plays fast enough that you can play multiple games in a short game night. The rulebook could use another editing, but the quality of the artwork is spectacular. All and all this was great addition to my collection (and a great surprise birthday present from my girlfriend).

Overall, I would give this a solid 7 out of 10. There are certainly games I would rather play if given the choice, but I also wouldn't be disappointed to get this on the table.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This has been compared with Web of Power and Showmanager and Ticket to Ride.  To me, it really only has slight similarities in mechanics with them.  It's a card (city-connection) collection game of trying to place all your houses on the board while making a series of city connections (carriages).  Getting houses on the cities are important as well as attaining bonus chits.  Cards are acquired via a draft board (i.e. Showmanager) with an option to select one of four officials or roles.  It's essentially a race of card collections with quirky house placement rules (i.e. Web of Power).  Whomever optimizes their course of making and laying efficient card sets will likely get the win.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
What a pity that the winner of the 2006 SdJ is so similar to the winner of 2004. Thurn and Taxis is a very solid game, of course, but it feels too much like Ticket to Ride without its elegant simplicity. By adding special powers and complexifying the scoring system with point tiles and carts, T&T gained in weight, which makes it more of a gamer's game, but it also lost that magic quality of attracting total newbies that T2R has. 

It's also harder to follow what's happening in the game because a lot of the action happens in the cards in front of each player and not just on the board. But I suppose in the end, since player interaction is so limited, it doesn't matter that much.

RATING: 8
The game was as good as advertised, although it really doesn't feel too much like [GAMEID=9209].  Compared to [GAMEID=9209], it's not quite as visceral---it's not as easy to pick up, it's not as confrontational, and it's not as gut wrenching as you're waiting for the right color card to come up to grab your route before someone else does.  Instead, it's a much more thoughtful game.  There's no direct blocking, although your opponent can take the card you're looking for.  Instead of completing tickets, the route building was a set up for an area control mechanism as you try to grab each region's bonus points first.  It's definitely an optimization, efficiency game and it works very well with two players.

Similar to [GAMEID=6249], we've never really had the urge to try out any of the expansions.  I guess after Carcassonne, we're just expansion'd out...

RATING: 8
The criticism that this game lacks player interaction, while founded, is overblown. The game's a race. You have do things before the other players do. If an opponent reaches a milestone before you do, you may have to shift to make up the point, and you have to be in flexible enough position to do so. This is also a game that rewards repeated play. If everyone's familiar, the game goes very, very quickly (20 minutes is possible), so the racing is really a sprint. Two players is almost as good a game as three or four, another big plus.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Bought UK Game Expo 2013. Very tight scoring, if you want to get ahead and finish the game early you need to make very good decisions regarding which helpers to make use of. Cartwright especially. Because of the tightness in scoring this feels more like a race game than a Euro. But I like it and it is a very good gateway game, albeit somewhat punishing if you make mistakes.

Update August 2014: So we had been scoring all of the cards you get, not realising you only score the longest route card. Now the game is a little more balanced and you can neglect the length of the routes a little more if you please in order to hoick as many tiles as possible instead. It makes for a much better game and is a lot more balanced. Still enjoying this game a lot.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Somewhat similar to Ticket to Ride but it does have its own flavour and variation in rules.  Instead of trains, players are trying to link horse and carriage taxi routes.  Scoring is mission based but also bonuses are awarded by territorial accomplishments.
